<div id="review_2098671393" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2098671393" />
    <a title="Tara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/70459127-tara"><img alt="Tara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592666421p2/70459127.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2098671393?book_show_action=true">Aug 20, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tara" href="/user/show/70459127-tara">Tara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140447958" href="/book/show/750487.A_Hero_of_Our_Time">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/70459127-tara?shelf=1001-list">1001-list</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2098671393" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10558572419894572894"><blockquote>A <i>Timeless</i> Hero</blockquote><br/>The sixth of June is the anniversary of D-Day, a day on which the Allies invaded Normandy in 1944. I overheard a conversation between two older gentlemen on the sixth of this month, interestingly enough the very day I began reading this book. They were discussing the Allied landings. Their talk eventually turned to subject matter you are no doubt familiar with: how “back in the old days,” men fought for what was truly important. How today’s youth are lost, adrift and purposeless. </span>
  <span id="freeText10558572419894572894" style="display:none"><blockquote>A <i>Timeless</i> Hero</blockquote><br />The sixth of June is the anniversary of D-Day, a day on which the Allies invaded Normandy in 1944. I overheard a conversation between two older gentlemen on the sixth of this month, interestingly enough the very day I began reading this book. They were discussing the Allied landings. Their talk eventually turned to subject matter you are no doubt familiar with: how “back in the old days,” men fought for what was truly important. How today’s youth are lost, adrift and purposeless. How modern young people don’t understand what it means to really sacrifice, to act bravely and selflessly for a worthy cause. <br /><br />What does all this have to do with <i>A Hero of Our Time</i>? Take a look at what the disillusioned protagonist has to say about <i>his</i> generation:<br /><blockquote>
  <i>“And we, their miserable descendants, roaming over the earth, without faith, without pride, without enjoyment, and without terror—except that involuntary awe which makes the heart shrink at the thought of the inevitable end—we are no longer capable of great sacrifices, either for the good of mankind or even for our own happiness, because we know the impossibility of such happiness; and, just as our ancestors used to fling themselves from one delusion to another, we pass indifferently from doubt to doubt, without possessing, as they did, either hope or even that vague though, at the same time, keen enjoyment which the soul encounters at every struggle with mankind or with destiny.”</i>
</blockquote><br />The Napoleonic Wars ended in 1815. What do the following generations, the generations who missed out on warfare of that magnitude, tend to feel about their own contributions, or lack thereof? As the tide of history ebbs and flows, the sentiments expressed so eloquently above must needs inevitably recur. This is not to say that feelings of emptiness, boredom, cynicism and despondency are not present in us all to some extent, regardless of the epoch in which we live. Far from it! (I’m rather too adept at cynicism myself.) But these feelings are certainly intensified during certain historical eras. Thus, a major influence on Lermontov’s hero was the sensation of ennui and malaise particular to a generation that is keenly aware of the fact that it has no great war, struggle or cause to fight for.<br /><br />The (anti-)hero in question, however, is far more complicated than that. Pechorin is a singularly compelling “Byronic hero,” memorable for his formidable mind and his tormented spirit. He certainly demonstrates his capacity for cruelty, for grossly malicious scheming, and yet the reader can’t really dislike him for it. One reason for this is perhaps the fact that he suffered from alienation in his formative years, and this rejection indelibly shaped and scarred him:<br /><blockquote>
  <i>“Yes, such has been my lot from very childhood! All have read upon my countenance the marks of bad qualities, which were not existent; but they were assumed to exist—and they were born. I was modest—I was accused of slyness: I grew secretive. I profoundly felt both good and evil—no one caressed me, all insulted me: I grew vindictive. I was gloomy—other children merry and talkative; I felt myself higher than they—I was rated lower: I grew envious. I was prepared to love the whole world—no one understood me: I learned to hate. My colourless youth flowed by in conflict with myself and the world; fearing ridicule, I buried my best feelings in the depths of my heart, and there they died. I spoke the truth—I was not believed: I began to deceive. Having acquired a thorough knowledge of the world and the springs of society, I grew skilled in the science of life; and I saw how others without skill were happy, enjoying gratuitously the advantages which I so unweariedly sought. Then despair was born within my breast—not that despair which is cured at the muzzle of a pistol, but the cold, powerless despair concealed beneath the mask of amiability and a good-natured smile. I became a moral cripple. One half of my soul ceased to exist; it dried up, evaporated, died, and I cut it off and cast it from me. The other half moved and lived—at the service of all; but it remained unobserved, because no one knew that the half which had perished had ever existed.”</i>
</blockquote> <br />Then, too, he is a remarkably strong, gifted, intelligent person, with no real outlet for his many talents. As he says himself, <i>“a genius, chained to an official desk, must die or go mad.”</i> This, in conjunction with the initial quote regarding Pechorin’s generation, brings to mind a rather fitting quote from <i>Fight Club</i>:<br /><blockquote>
  <i>“I see in fight club the strongest and smartest men who've ever lived. I see all this potential and I see squandering. Goddamn it, an entire generation pumping gas, waiting tables, slaves with white collars, advertising has us chasing cars and clothes, working jobs we hate so we can buy shit we don't need. We're the middle children of history, man, no purpose or place, we have no Great war, no Great depression, our great war is a spiritual war, our great depression is our lives, we've been all raised by television to believe that one day we'd all be millionaires and movie gods and rock stars, but we won't and we're slowly learning that fact. and we're very, very pissed off.”</i>
</blockquote> <br />And I’ve mentioned but a few of the numerous elements that combine to make our hero so fascinating, so real, so very <i>human</i>. In many ways, Lermontov’s hero is truly timeless, and hence a highly important creation. This type of individual reappears time and again throughout the course of our history. That is why he is so relatable. That is why he continues to matter.<br /><blockquote>
  <i>“…my imagination is unquiet, my heart insatiate. To me everything is of little moment. I become as easily accustomed to grief as to joy, and my life grows emptier day by day.”</i>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="10558572419894572894" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1287412722" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1287412722" />
    <a title="Junta" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38230974-junta"><img alt="Junta" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1447099175p2/38230974.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1287412722?book_show_action=true">May 22, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Junta" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Junta" href="/user/show/38230974-junta">Junta</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143105633" href="/book/show/5986287-a-hero-of-our-time">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fans of Russian classics or Byronic Heroes</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Junta by: </span>
      My father
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38230974-junta?shelf=translated">translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38230974-junta?shelf=russian">russian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1287412722" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6690349265285322453"><blockquote>With his book, the young Lermontov, only twenty-six when he wrote <i>A Hero of Our Time</i>, called the Russian novel into being...In turn, Chekhov, Dostoyevsky, and Tolstoy were each influenced by Lermontov - his metaphysics, his ironic stances, and his descriptions of the Caucasus. - Natasha Randall<br/><br/>It is arguably true that Lermontov had a more lasting impact on the shape and contours of the Russian novel than either Pushkin or Gogol. <i>A Hero of Our Time</i> possesses three of the most central characterist</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText6690349265285322453" style="display:none"><blockquote>With his book, the young Lermontov, only twenty-six when he wrote <i>A Hero of Our Time</i>, called the Russian novel into being...In turn, Chekhov, Dostoyevsky, and Tolstoy were each influenced by Lermontov - his metaphysics, his ironic stances, and his descriptions of the Caucasus. - Natasha Randall<br /><br />It is arguably true that Lermontov had a more lasting impact on the shape and contours of the Russian novel than either Pushkin or Gogol. <i>A Hero of Our Time</i> possesses three of the most central characteristics of the Russian novel: 1) psychological analysis; 2) concern with ideas; 3) sociopolitical and ethical awareness. - John Garrard<br /></blockquote> - from the <i>Introduction</i><br /><br />The plot is of relative unimportance in this novel - instead, the reader's attention becomes glued to the title character, the main protagonist, in this case Grigory Alexandrovich Pechorin (derived from <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pechora_River" rel="nofollow noopener">the Pechora River</a>, as homage to Pushkin's <i>Eugene Onegin</i> (<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Onega_River" rel="nofollow noopener">the Onega River</a>)). Pechorin, an embodiment of the Byronic Hero, lives with deep contradictions: he is charismatic, intelligent, seductive and cynical; yet is also intellectually tortured, reckless, depressive and sensitive. The following quote, uttered by Pechorin, gives us a clear picture of the man (hidden for its length):<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  <blockquote>I have an unfortunate character - whether it is how I was brought up, or whether God created me this way, I don't know. I only know that if I am the cause of unhappiness in others, then I am no less unhappy myself. In my early youth, from the moment I left the care of my parents, I began furiously enjoying all the many pleasures you can obtain for money, and then, it seems, these pleasures became loathsome to me. Then I set forth into the wide world, and soon I'd had enough of society too. I fell in love with society beauties and was loved by them too - but their love only inflamed my imagination and pride, leaving my heart empty...I started to read, to study - but academics also bored me. I realized that neither glory nor happiness depends on them, because the happiest people are the ignorant. Glory comes from good fortune, and to attain it, you must merely be cunning. And then everything became tedious...Soon after, they transferred me to the Caucasus: this was the happiest time of my life. I hoped that boredom didn't exist under Chechen bullets, but it was in vain - within a month I was so used to their whirring and to the nearness of death, that really, I paid more attention to the mosquitoes. And I was more bored than before, because I had lost what was nearly my last hope. When I saw Bela in my home, when for the first time I held her in my knees, I kissed her black curls, like a fool, I thought that she was an angel, sent to me by compassionate Fate...I was again mistaken. The love of a savage girl is not much better than the love of a noblewoman. The ignorance and simple-heartedness of the one becomes as tiresome as the coquettishness of the other. If you like, I still love her, I am grateful to her for several sufficiently sweet minutes. I would give my life for her, only I am bored in her company...Whether I'm a fool or a scoundrel, I don't know. But one thing is sure - that I am as worthy of pity, maybe even more so, as she is. The soul inside me is corrupted by the world, my imagination is restless, my heart is insatiable. Nothing is ever enough. I have become as used to sorrow as I am to delight, and my life becomes more empty from one day to the next. There is only one remedy left for me: travel. As soon as I can, I will set off - only not to Europe, God forbid! I'll go to America, to Arabia, to India - maybe I'll perish somewhere along the way! At least, I am certain, that this final solace will not be exhausted too quickly, with the help of storms and bad roads.</blockquote>
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />The layout of the novel is an interesting one - in the first third, the parts <i>Bela</i> and <i>Maxim Maximych</i>, the narrator (and we as the reader) hears about Pechorin through other people recounting episodes from the past, in the Caucasus, which as the setting is the other nuclei of this novel, besides Pechorin - life and imagery in the Caucasus is described by Lermontov in detail and, we can sense, admiration. In the other three parts, we read excerpts of Pechorin's diary in <i>Taman</i>, <i>Princess Mary</i> and <i>The Fatalist</i>. The five parts of the novel are read in the order A-B-C-D-E, but chronologically, these occur in the order C-D-A-E-B, and thus only by reading the latter parts do we understand, or glimpse an idea of why Pechorin is the way he is when we see him from a third person view in the earlier parts.<br /><br />The highlight of this novel for me was the relationship between the Hero, Pechorin, and the author, Lermontov.<blockquote>What is eerie is that not only did Pechorin and Lermontov share experiences and a troubled sense of purpose, but there is a duel scene in <i>A Hero of Our Time</i> that almost perfectly describes Lermontov's own death a year or so after the novel emerged.</blockquote> - Natasha Randall, from the <i>Introduction</i><br /><br />As Dostoyevsky puts his heart and soul into the portrayal of the depraved man, The Underground Man, in his stories, so Lermontov brings to us the superfluous hero, the Byronic Hero. With the cover of this Penguin edition of the novel, I had been thinking it was a portrait of Pechorin the whole time, but when I finished the novel and read the back cover, my folly was realised - it was Lermontov. And in that moment, the parallels between the two were magnified in my mind, and I was left thinking: how tragic that Lermontov was killed in a duel at such a young age, at 26! How many more masterful stories he could have written, perhaps charting a different course for Russian literature...<br /><br />Talking about this Penguin edition, I was impressed by Randall's informative and engrossing introduction, while I thought the foreword by Neil LaBute was frankly unnecessary.<br /><br />I haven't really touched on what happens in the novel - but what was important for me was the character of Pechorin. His exploits, adventures and charades in the army, in society, the Caucasus...they are mere extensions of who he is as a person. He has flaws, he can be ruthless, he can be tragic...but I am a fan of the Byronic Hero. I must go on and read Lermontov's poems in the near future, and I can recommend <i>A Hero of Our Time</i> to those who are already acquainted with Russian literature.<br /><br />September 14, 2015</span>
  <a data-text-id="6690349265285322453" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_94655233" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/94655233" />
    <a title="Selena" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/128546-selena"><img alt="Selena" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528898811p2/128546.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/94655233?book_show_action=true">Mar 18, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Selena" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Selena" href="/user/show/128546-selena">Selena</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780812970760" href="/book/show/27615.A_Hero_of_Our_Time">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/128546-selena?shelf=2010">2010</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/128546-selena?shelf=thebest">thebest</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/128546-selena?shelf=mother-russia">mother-russia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer94655233" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1782525149734173172">For a brief period after Pushkin’s death, Lermontov was Russia’s noted writer. He was the Byron of his time, with his romanticized ideals, novel without plot featuring his noted antihero. I’ve returned lately to Nabokov and am taking his advice on what to read (oddly, his favourites re-affirm the suggestions friends have lately given me).<br/><br/>Nabokov writes:<br/><br/>It would seem that all the veneration elderly critics have for A Hero is rather a glorified recollection of youthful readings in the twilight, an</span>
  <span id="freeText1782525149734173172" style="display:none">For a brief period after Pushkin’s death, Lermontov was Russia’s noted writer. He was the Byron of his time, with his romanticized ideals, novel without plot featuring his noted antihero. I’ve returned lately to Nabokov and am taking his advice on what to read (oddly, his favourites re-affirm the suggestions friends have lately given me).<br /><br />Nabokov writes:<br /><br />It would seem that all the veneration elderly critics have for A Hero is rather a glorified recollection of youthful readings in the twilight, and of ardent self-identification, than the direct result of a mature consciousness of art.<br /><br />It doesn’t seem like a compliment but in a way, A Hero of Our Time is the Russian version of Catcher in the Rye, one of those books you’re supposed to read at a certain time in your life, in your youth. I’m not particularly young but A Hero of Our Time resonated with me so strongly.<br /><br />It began in the author’s preface, before I even got to the text. Lermontov is unapologetic about his words, he dares to throw them at you without worry or care.<br /><br />Pardon me. Enough of feeding people sweets; this has spoiled their digestion: we need bitter medicine and caustic truths.<br /><br />Pechorin’s story is told in three parts. First through the eyes of a former comrade, second the eyes of a fascinated onlooker who by chance inherits his journals, and lastly through his own words. Many who’ve read the book claim that the first two sections of the novels don’t hold as much interest as that of the third, the section told through Grigory Pechorin’s eyes but I disagree.<br /><br />It is only by looking at the three vignettes as pieces to a whole that the reader gets a feel for Pechorin’s motivations, who he is as a person and how he comes off as a character to others. In fact, without those first two sections, his story wouldn’t have as much meaning or significance, in my eyes.<br /><br />The order of the vignettes felt particularly important. With each new narrator, layers of Pechorin’s tough exterior peeled away until we got to truly see him through his own eyes. Sometimes it was difficult to think about Pechorin’s views, much like Lermontov warns us in the beginning – it is bitter medicine and caustic truths.<br /><br />Throughout the novel I found many references to the great Lord Byron. In some ways, it seemed like every great character within the novel featured “Byronic” traits. It was strangely satisfying to notice the tiny references embedded within the text.<br /><br />Once time has passed and my feelings for the book have settled (perhaps once I’ve read it again), I’ll have the words to truly explain my fast feelings toward this novel. In the meantime…<br /><br />Nicole of Bibliographing wisely says “Take your Darcys and Rochesters, your Heathcliffs and your Werthers; give me Pechorin any day.”<br /><br />Amen, amen, amen.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1782525149734173172" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1618476260" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1618476260" />
    <a title="Kartik" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31291929-kartik"><img alt="Kartik" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1478617541p2/31291929.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1618476260?book_show_action=true">Apr 23, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kartik" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kartik" href="/user/show/31291929-kartik">Kartik</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143105633" href="/book/show/5986287-a-hero-of-our-time">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1618476260" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13114255177989477806">This novel... Wow. <br/><br/>Hailed as a seminal influence on the works of subsequent Russian authors such as Tolstoy, Dostoyevsky, and Chekhov, A Hero Of Our Time effortlessly straddles both Romanticism and Realism, a style still in its nascence during Lermontov's own time. The book revolves around Pechorin, the hero the title refers to, a Russian soldier posted to the Caucasus, who embodies the spirit of the Byronic hero, in its Russian form : The superfluous man - Aristocratic, brooding, licentious, cr</span>
  <span id="freeText13114255177989477806" style="display:none">This novel... Wow. <br /><br />Hailed as a seminal influence on the works of subsequent Russian authors such as Tolstoy, Dostoyevsky, and Chekhov, A Hero Of Our Time effortlessly straddles both Romanticism and Realism, a style still in its nascence during Lermontov's own time. The book revolves around Pechorin, the hero the title refers to, a Russian soldier posted to the Caucasus, who embodies the spirit of the Byronic hero, in its Russian form : The superfluous man - Aristocratic, brooding, licentious, crushed by existential boredom, distrustful of authority and social propriety, cynical to a fault, and vain and prideful. In fact, his character serves as a prototype for the archetype itself. 'There isn't a person in the world over whom the past gains such power as it does over me,' he declares, at one point.<br /><br />(There are numerous references to both Lord Byron's and Sir Walter Scott's work in the book.)<br /><br />We learn of Pechorin first through exploits and escapades told second hand, and later we learn the passions that drive him from the second half of the book, written in the first person. This makes for an interesting contrast, because at first we see Pechorin through his actions, and then later through his thoughts - Basically, the contrast between how other people see him and how he sees himself.<br /><br />Just when the narrative starts getting cynical and weary with the weight of the world, Lermontov's narrators bring back the spirit of Romanticism in his breath-taking descriptions of the Caucasus and life there. Some of the more lyrical passages even talk about the promise of self transcendence, relevant even today, in this era of soul crushing ennui.<br /><br />Something of interest, something that the book actually focuses a lot on, is Pechorin's attitudes towards women. He both humanizes and objectifies them, but ultimately romanticizes them, believing that they 'have an instinct for the beautiful soul'. Pechorin's escapades ultimately end up bringing his own insecurities and issues to the forefront.<br /><br />Ultimately, I think Pechorin represents that side of young masculinity that constantly fights against inertia, craves love but is too proud to admit it, puts on a strong face while being very vulnerable, and is cautious of the power of emotions. Lermontov's portrait of Pechorin describes all these various facets of his personality and how they interact, creating someone larger than the sum of his parts, a character most younger men would be intimately familiar with already. The quote from the foreward - "one of the most vivid and persuasive portraits of the male ego ever put down on paper," sums it up perfectly.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13114255177989477806" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

		    <b>
          <a title="Reading Russian Literature 2020" class="actionLink" href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/21124197-discussion-on-a-hero-of-our-time">Reading Russian L...:</a>
        </b>
      
			<a class="actionLink" href="/topic/show/21124197-discussion-on-a-hero-of-our-time">Discussion on A Hero of our Time</a>
		</td>
		<td class="nobreak">

	<tr>
		<td>
		    <b>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('B/LKa5+OmfHheDFgVrg8hXq4zDUdaGAmi5yUayGgf4A91KO5JNBQmUZThQGhTTZarOcr/dPl7kHQ4tLa7OONGg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Es5bHk7CHP/pJXx9al4vndkdtj5L5LY22xxeK84gm6oo6DLM9ZzVl04OyBydqyVCD0JR9oVpOFGAYhiaA2NpMA==')})
  }

Set in imperial Russia during the 1820s, Pushkin\'s novel in verse follows the emotions and destiny of three men - Onegin the bored fop, Lensky the minor elegiast, and a stylized Pushkin himself - and the fates and affections of three women - Tatyana the provincial beauty, her sister Olga, and Pushkin\'s mercurial Muse. Engaging, full of suspense, and varied in tone, it also portrays a large cast of other characters and offers the reader many literary, philosophical, and autobiographical digressions, often in a highly satirical vein. Eugene Onegin was Pushkin\'s own favourite work, and it shows him attempting to transform himself from romantic poet into realistic novelist. This new translation seeks to retain both the literal sense and the poetic music of the original, and capture the poem\'s spontaneity and wit. Some of these problems included the uselessness of landowners and gentry in a feudal society that did not encourage innovation or reform, the complex relations between members of different classes of The novel\'s vision of Soviet life in the 1930s is so ferociously accurate that it could not be published during its author\'s lifetime and appeared only in a censored edition in the 1960s. Its truths are so enduring that its language has become part of the common Russian speech.\n\nOne hot spring, the devil arrives in Moscow, accompanied by a retinue that includes a beautiful naked witch and an immense talking black cat with a fondness for chess and vodka. The visitors quickly wreak havoc in a city that refuses to believe in either God or Satan. But they also bring peace to two unhappy Muscovites: one is the Master, a writer pilloried for daring to write a novel about Christ and Pontius Pilate; the other is Margarita, who loves the Master so deeply that she is willing literally to go to hell for him. What ensues is a novel of inexhaustible energy, humor, and philosophical depth, a work whose nuances emerge for the first time in Diana Burgin\'s and Katherine Tiernan O\'Connor\'s splendid English <span id="freeTextContainer9571841339090181731"><b>Mikhail Yuryevich Lermontov</b> (<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/14162769.________________________' title='Михаил Юрьевич Лермонтов' rel='nofollow noopener'>Михаил Юрьевич Лермонтов</a>), a Russian Romantic writer, poet and painter, sometimes called "the poet of the Caucasus", was the most important Russian poet after Alexander Pushkin's death. His influence on later Russian literature is still felt in modern times, not only through his poetry, but also by his prose.<br/><br/>Lermontov died in a duel like his great predecessor poet, Ale</span>
  <span id="freeText9571841339090181731" style="display:none"><b>Mikhail Yuryevich Lermontov</b> (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/14162769.________________________" title="Михаил Юрьевич Лермонтов" rel="nofollow noopener">Михаил Юрьевич Лермонтов</a>), a Russian Romantic writer, poet and painter, sometimes called "the poet of the Caucasus", was the most important Russian poet after Alexander Pushkin's death. His influence on later Russian literature is still felt in modern times, not only through his poetry, but also by his prose.<br /><br />Lermontov died in a duel like his great predecessor poet, Aleksandr Pushkin. <br />Even more so tragically strange (if not to say fatalistic) that both poets described in their major works fatal duel outcomes, in which the main characters (Onegin and Pechorin) were coming out victorious.<br /></span>

    go = function () {
      for (i = 0; i < n; i += 1) {
        script = d.createElement('SCRIPT');
        script.type = 'text/javascript';
        script.async = true;
        script.src = load[i];
        first.parentNode.insertBefore(script, first);
      }
    }
  if (w.attachEvent) {
    w.attachEvent('onload', go);
  } else {
    w.addEventListener('load', go, false);
  }
}(window, document,

     '//apis.google.com/js/plusone.js'
    ]
  ));

</script>
